        <meta property="og:url" content="https://pubs.acs.org/doi/full/10.1021/jacs.4c09379"><meta property="og:title" content="Local Electronic Structure Regulation Enabling Fluorophosphates Cathode with Improved Redox Potential and Reversible Capacity for Sodium-Ion Batteries"><meta property="og:image" content="https://pubs.acs.org/cms/10.1021/jacs.4c09379/asset/images/jacs.4c09379.social.jpeg_v03"><meta property="og:description" content="Mn-based fluorophosphates have attracted much attention as cathodes for sodium-ion batteries owing to their high cost effectiveness, considerable capacity, and stable framework. However, the fascinating Mn3+/2+ redox couple suffers from inadequate activation due to the Mn–O covalent character and poor electronic conductivity, impeding its further applications. Herein, a local electronic structure regulation strategy is proposed to improve the Mn3+/2+ redox potential and reversible capacity simultaneously through introducing elements with low-energy 3d orbitals to expand the energy gap between the eg orbitals and Fermi energy of Na. Moreover, the 3d element substitution serves to narrow the band gap toward the improved intrinsic electronic conductivity. In comparison with pristine Na2Fe0.45Mn0.55PO4F, the as-prepared Na2Fe0.45Mn0.4V0.1PO4F cathode achieves an increase from 3.5 to 3.6 V in the high-voltage platform and an improvement in energy density from 330 to 361 Wh kg–1. This work inspires new ideas in adjusting the redox potential of polyanionic cathodes through deliberate regulation of the local electronic structure."><meta property="og:type" content="Article"><meta property="og:site_name" content="ACS Publications"><meta name="twitter:card" content="summary_large_image"><meta name="twitter:site" content="@ACSPublications"><meta name="twitter:image" content="https://pubs.acs.org/cms/10.1021/jacs.4c09379/asset/images/jacs.4c09379.social.jpeg_v03">

<!-- loading Javascript for using the persistence Service -->
    <script src="/wro/1122814~thiss-ds.js" async="" nonce="9318aed9e81388d3-SJC"></script>

    <script type="text/javascript" nonce="9318aed9e81388d3-SJC">

        var SeamlessAccessService = {
            spUrl: "/action/ssostart",
            returnURL: window.location.href,
            my_context: 'seamlessaccess.org',
            timeout: 5000,

            getWAYFLessUrl: function (entityId) {
                return this.spUrl + "?idp=" + encodeURIComponent(entityId)
                    + "&redirectUri=" + encodeURIComponent(this.returnURL);
            },
            retrieveEntity: function (ms) {
                var self = this;
                var persistService = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                if (ms == undefined || ms === '') {
                    ms = this.timeout;
                }

                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    persistService.entities(self.my_context)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            return resolve(res.data);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            reject('failed to retrieve entity from local storage: ' + err);
                        });
                    // Set up the timeout
                    setTimeout(function () {
                        reject('Promise timed out ' + ms + ' ms');
                    }, ms);
                });
            },
            updateEntity: function (entitydata) {
                var self = this;
                var persistService = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                var jsonObj = {};
                try {
                    //try to parse it, if it is a json string
                    jsonObj = JSON.parse(entitydata.val());
                } catch (e) {
                    jsonObj = entitydata;
                }
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    persistService.update(self.my_context, jsonObj)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // Process the results
                            var ssoInstitutions = res.data;
                            return resolve(ssoInstitutions);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to update the local storage due to: ' + err);
                            reject(err);
                        });
                })
            },
            removeEntity: function (entityId) {
                var self = this;
                var persistService = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    persistService.remove(self.my_context, entityId)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            return resolve(res);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to remove from local storage duo to: ' + err);
                        });
                })
            },
            getAccessibleEntityStatus: function (doi, entityId, entityTitle) {
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    $.ajax({
                        type: 'POST',
                        url: '/action/seamlessAccess',
                        accept: {
                            text: "application/json"
                        },
                        data: {
                            doi: doi,
                            entityid: entityId,
                            entityTitle: entityTitle,
                            eventGroupKey: '28889f4c-b187-47d0-80e1-6eb28f2d9b09'
                        }

                    }).done(function (status) {
                        return resolve(status);
                    }).fail(function (status) {
                        return reject(status);
                    });
                });
            },
            getEntitiesWithAllInfo: function (doi) {
                let self = this,
                    arrayOfPromises = [];
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    self.retrieveEntity().then(function (res) {
                        let allSsoInstitutions = res;
                        for (i = 0; i < allSsoInstitutions.length; i++) {
                            let ssoInstitution = allSsoInstitutions[i];
                            arrayOfPromises.push(new Promise(function (resolve) {
                                self.getAccessibleEntityStatus(doi, ssoInstitution.entity.entityID, ssoInstitution.entity.title)
                                    .then(function (entityStatus) {
                                        resolve({
                                            entityID: ssoInstitution.entity.entityID,
                                            entityTitle: ssoInstitution.entity.title,
                                            entityStatusObj: entityStatus,
                                            entitySsoUrl: self.getWAYFLessUrl(ssoInstitution.entity.entityID)
                                        });
                                    }, function (err) {
                                        console.log('failed to get the entity status: ' + ssoInstitution.entity.entityID,err);
                                    });
                            }));
                        }
                        if(allSsoInstitutions.length === 0 && true){
                            self.getAccessibleEntityStatus(doi, null, null)
                                .then(function () {
                                    console.log('No Entity found');
                                }, function (err) {
                                    console.log('failed to get the none entity status' ,err);
                                });
                        }
                        Promise.all(arrayOfPromises)
                            .then(function (finalResponse) {
                                resolve(finalResponse);
                            }).catch(function (err) {
                                reject(err);
                            });

                    }).catch(function (err) {
                        reject(err);
                    })
                });
            }
        };

        window.addEventListener('load', function () {
            document.querySelectorAll(".sso-institution").forEach(function (institution) {
                var entityId = institution.getAttribute('data-entityid');
                var entityName = institution.getAttribute('data-name');
                var entitydata = {
                    entityID: entityId,
                    title: entityName
                };
                institution.addEventListener("click", function (e) {
                    e.preventDefault();

                    if ($('.institution-preference-userconsent-checkbox').length > 0) {
                        let institutionPreferenceUserConsentCheckbox = $('.institution-preference-userconsent-checkbox').get(0);
                        if (!$(institutionPreferenceUserConsentCheckbox).is(":checked")) {
                            return;
                        }
                    }

                    var self = this;
                    SeamlessAccessService.updateEntity(entitydata).then(function (res) {
                        console.log(res);
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    }).catch(function (err) {
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    });
                });
            });
        });

    </script>

<script type="text/javascript" nonce="9318aed9e81388d3-SJC">
var dfpReady = false;
$(document).ready(function() {
    // var journalTitle = 'JACS';
    var taxonomyKeywords = $('.article_header-taxonomy');
    var articleKeyword = $('meta[name="keywords"]');
    var articleTitle = $('meta[name="dc.Title"]');
    var articleDescription = $('meta[name="dc.Description"]');
    var abstractKeyword = $('p.articleBody_abstractText:contains("Keywords: ")');
    var searchTerms = [];

    if (taxonomyKeywords.length > 0) {
        $(taxonomyKeywords).children(".rlist--inline").children("li").each(function() {
            searchTerms.push($(this).text());
        });
    }
    else if (articleKeyword.length > 0) { 
        searchTerms = searchTerms.concat(articleKeyword.prop('content').split(', '));
    }
    else if (abstractKeyword.length > 0) {
        searchTerms = searchTerms.concat(abstractKeyword.text().split("Keywords: ")[1].split('; '));
    }
    else if (articleDescription.length > 0) {
        $(articleDescription).each(function() {
            articleDescriptionKeywords = $(this).prop('content');
            articleDescriptionKeywords = articleDescriptionKeywords.replace(/.,/g,"");
            articleDescriptionKeywords = articleDescriptionKeywords.replace(/\s{2,}/g," ");
            searchTerms = searchTerms.concat(articleDescriptionKeywords.split(' '));
        });
    }

    if (articleTitle.length > 0) {
        searchTerms = searchTerms.concat(articleTitle.prop('content').split(' '));
    }

    googletag.cmd.push(function() {
        var mappingLeader = googletag.sizeMapping().
        addSize([750, 200], [728, 90]). 
        addSize([0, 0], [320, 50]).
        build();
        //leaderboard
        googletag.defineSlot('/8868/journal/JACS/article', [728, 90], 'dfp-journal-article-leaderboard').defineSizeMapping(mappingLeader).setTargeting('location', 'leaderboard1').setTargeting('Keyword', searchTerms).addService(googletag.pubads());
        //recommend
        googletag.defineSlot("/8868/journal/JACS/article", [1, 1], "dfp-journal-article-recommend").setTargeting("ntvPlacement", ["1100167"]).setTargeting("location", "recommend").setTargeting('Keyword', searchTerms).addService(googletag.pubads());
        //googletag.pubads().enableSingleRequest();
        googletag.pubads().collapseEmptyDivs();
        googletag.pubads().setTargeting('inst',dartInstitutionType).setTargeting('memb',dartMember);
        googletag.pubads().addEventListener("slotRenderEnded", function(event) {
            console.log(event.slot.getSlotElementId() + " isEmpty: " + event.isEmpty + " | creative id: " + event.creativeId + " " + event.size[0] + "x" + event.size[1]);
            if((event.isEmpty === false) && (event.slot.getSlotElementId() != "dfp-journal-article-recommend")) {
                $("#" + event.slot.getSlotElementId()).parents(".advertisement").slideDown();
                console.log("ad display: " + event.creativeId + " " + event.size[0] + "x" + event.size[1]);
            }
            // else {
            //     console.log("no ad found");
            // }
        });
        googletag.enableServices();
        dfpReady = true;
    });
});
</script>

data-references="https://pubs.acs.org/action/ajaxShowEnhancedAbstract?doi=10.1021%2Fjacs.4c09379&amp;ajax=true" data-enable-mathjax="true" vocab="http://schema.org/" typeof="ScholarlyArticle" class="article--latest"><div class="figure-viewer"><div class="figure-viewer_row"><div class="figure-viewer_col loop-trap"><div aria-live="polite" aria-atomic="true" class="figure-viewer_modal"><a href="#" title="Close" aria-label="close" tabindex="0" class="figure-viewer_close icon-close"></a><h2 class="figure-viewer_label"><span class="figure-viewer_current"></span></h2><div href="/action/showCitFormats?doi=10.1021/jacs.4c09379&amp;href=/doi/10.1021/jacs.4c09379" title="Cite This" class="figure-viewer-cite-this-link"><i class="icon-check-circle"></i><span><strong>Cite <script nonce="9318aed9e81388d3-SJC">
$(function() {
    var serialCode = "jacsat";
    var books = ["reagents", "symposium", "advances", "medichemreviews", "styleguide"]
    if(books.includes(serialCode) === false) {
        // default click action
        $("[data-id]").on("mousedown", function(e) {
            var currentClickID = $(this).attr("data-id");

            if(currentClickID.includes("_hover") === false) {
                if(currentClickID.match("^article_") && !currentClickID.match("^article_abstract_CC")) {   
                    gtag('event', "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Local Electronic Structure Regulation Enabling Fluorophosphates Cathode with Improved Redox Potential and Reversible Capacity for Sodium-Ion Batteries", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/jacs.4c09379", "Article_Element": $(this).attr("data-id") });
                }
            }
        });
        
        // Access Management icons hover
        var articleAccessManagementIconHover = false;
        var articleAccessManagementIconHoverEvent = false;
        $("[data-id='article_access_icon']").on("mouseenter", function(e) {
            if(articleAccessManagementIconHoverEvent === false) {
                articleAccessManagementIconHover = true;
                var articleAccessManagementHover =  $(this).attr("data-id") + "_hover";
                
                setTimeout(function() { 
                    if(articleAccessManagementIconHover === true) {
                        gtag("event", "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Local Electronic Structure Regulation Enabling Fluorophosphates Cathode with Improved Redox Potential and Reversible Capacity for Sodium-Ion Batteries", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/jacs.4c09379", "Article_Element": articleAccessManagementHover });
    
                        articleAccessManagementIconHoverEvent = true;
                    }
                }, 1000);
            }
        });
        $("[data-id='article_access_icon']").on("mouseleave", function(e) {
            articleAccessManagementIconHover = false;
        });
    
        // articleRightsPermissionsLink hover
        var articleRightsPermissionsLinkHover = false;
        var articleRightsPermissionsLinkHoverEvent = false;
        $("[data-id='article_rights_permissions']").on("mouseenter", function(e) {
            if(articleRightsPermissionsLinkHoverEvent === false) {
                articleRightsPermissionsLinkHover = true;
                var articleRightsHover =  $(this).attr("data-id") + "_hover";
                
                setTimeout(function() { 
                    if(articleRightsPermissionsLinkHover === true) {
                        gtag("event", "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Local Electronic Structure Regulation Enabling Fluorophosphates Cathode with Improved Redox Potential and Reversible Capacity for Sodium-Ion Batteries", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/jacs.4c09379", "Article_Element": articleRightsHover });
    
                        articleRightsPermissionsLinkHoverEvent = true;
                    }
                }, 1000);
            }
        });
        $("[data-id='article_rights_permissions']").on("mouseleave", function(e) {
            articleRightsPermissionsLinkHover = false;
        });
    
        // Author hover
        var articleAuthorHover = false
        var articleAuthorHoverEvent = false
        $("[data-id='article_author_info']").on("mouseenter", function(e) {
            if((articleAuthorHoverEvent === false) && ($(this).parent().hasClass("view-affs") === false)) {
                articleAuthorHover = true;
                var authorInfoHover =  $(this).attr("data-id") + "_hover";
                
                setTimeout(function() { 
                    if((articleAuthorHover === true) && (articleAuthorHoverEvent === false)) {
                        gtag("event", "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Local Electronic Structure Regulation Enabling Fluorophosphates Cathode with Improved Redox Potential and Reversible Capacity for Sodium-Ion Batteries", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/jacs.4c09379", "Article_Element": authorInfoHover });
    
                        articleAuthorHoverEvent = true;
                    }
                }, 1000);
            }
        });
        $("[data-id='article_author_info']").on("mouseleave", function(e) {
            articleAuthorHover = false;
        });
    
        // CC License hover
        var articleAbstractCCHover = false
        var articleAbstractCCHoverEvent = false
        var articleDrawerCCHover = false
        var articleDrawerCCHoverEvent = false
        $(".article__cc-license__inter a").on("mouseenter", function(e) {
            if($(this).attr("data-id")) {
                if(($(this).attr("data-id") === "article_drawer_CC-License") && (articleDrawerCCHoverEvent === false)) {
                    articleDrawerCCHover = true;
                    
                    setTimeout(function() { 
                        if((articleDrawerCCHover === true) && (articleDrawerCCHoverEvent === false)) {
                            gtag("event", "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Local Electronic Structure Regulation Enabling Fluorophosphates Cathode with Improved Redox Potential and Reversible Capacity for Sodium-Ion Batteries", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/jacs.4c09379", "Article_Element": "article_drawer_CC-License_hover" });
    
                            articleDrawerCCHoverEvent = true;
                        }
                    }, 1000);
                }
                else if(($(this).attr("data-id").match("^article_abstract_CC")) && (articleAbstractCCHoverEvent === false)) {
                    articleAbstractCCHover = true;
    
                    setTimeout(function() { 
                        if((articleAbstractCCHover === true) && (articleAbstractCCHoverEvent === false)) {
                            gtag("event", "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Local Electronic Structure Regulation Enabling Fluorophosphates Cathode with Improved Redox Potential and Reversible Capacity for Sodium-Ion Batteries", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/jacs.4c09379", "Article_Element": "article_abstract_CC_hover" });
    
                            articleAbstractCCHoverEvent = true;
                        }
                    }, 1000);
                }
            }
            else if(($(this).hasClass("article__cc-license__link")) && (articleAbstractCCHoverEvent === false)) {
                articleAbstractCCHover = true;
                
                setTimeout(function() { 
                    if((articleAbstractCCHover === true) && (articleAbstractCCHoverEvent === false)) {
                        gtag("event", "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Local Electronic Structure Regulation Enabling Fluorophosphates Cathode with Improved Redox Potential and Reversible Capacity for Sodium-Ion Batteries", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/jacs.4c09379", "Article_Element": "article_abstract_CC_hover" });
    
                        articleAbstractCCHoverEvent = true;
                    }
                }, 1000);
            }
        });
        $(".article__cc-license__inter a").on("mouseleave", function(e) {
            if(articleAbstractCCHover === true) {
                articleAbstractCCHover = false;
            }
            if(articleDrawerCCHover = true) {
                articleDrawerCCHover = false;
            }
        });
        
        // show recommeneded click delay
        var intervalCount = 0;
        var showRecommendedInterval = setInterval(function() {
            intervalCount += 100;
            if($(".show-recommended__wrapper .show-recommended").length > 0) {
                clearInterval(showRecommendedInterval);
                $("[data-id='article_drawer_RecommendedShowMore']").on("mousedown", function(e) {
                    gtag('event', "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Local Electronic Structure Regulation Enabling Fluorophosphates Cathode with Improved Redox Potential and Reversible Capacity for Sodium-Ion Batteries", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/jacs.4c09379", "Article_Element": $(this).attr("data-id") });
                });
                $("[data-id='article_drawer_RecommendedShowLess']").on("mousedown", function(e) {
                    gtag('event', "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Local Electronic Structure Regulation Enabling Fluorophosphates Cathode with Improved Redox Potential and Reversible Capacity for Sodium-Ion Batteries", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/jacs.4c09379", "Article_Element": $(this).attr("data-id") });
                });
            }
        }, 100);
    }
});
</script>

class="articleMetrics_count"><h2>Citations</h2><div class="articleMetrics-val"><a href="#citeThis" class="internalNav">11</a></div></div></div></div><div class="articleMetrics_about"><div class="base data-db-target-of="aboutArticleMetrics" id="about-article-metrics" class="dropBlock__holder"><button class="articleMetrics_close"> <i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-close_thin"></i><span class="sr-only">close</span></button><p>Article Views are the COUNTER-compliant sum of full text article downloads since November 2008 (both PDF and HTML) across all institutions and individuals. These metrics are regularly updated to reflect usage leading up to the last few days.</p><p>Citations are the number of other articles citing this article, calculated by Crossref and updated daily.<a href="https://www.crossref.org/services/cited-by/"> Find more information about Crossref citation counts.</a></p><p>The Altmetric Attention Score is a quantitative measure of the attention that a research article has received online. Clicking on the donut icon will load a page at altmetric.com with additional details about the score and the social media presence for the given article. Find more information on<a href="https://help.altmetric.com/support/solutions/articles/6000233311-how-is-the-altmetric-attention-score-calculated"> the Altmetric Attention Score and how the score is calculated.</a></p></div></div></div></div></div></div></div></div><div class="article_abstract"><div class="article_abstract-title" id="Abstract" tabindex="0"><h2>Abstract</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div><div class="article_abstract-content hlFld-Abstract" id="abstractBox" property="description"><figure id="_i1" data-index="0" class="article__inlineFigure article_abstract-img"><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img class="inline-fig internalNav" src="/cms/10.1021/jacs.4c09379/asset/images/medium/ja4c09379_0006.gif" alt="" id="tgr1" property="image" style="width:405px; height:275px"></button><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/jacs.4c09379/asset/images/large/ja4c09379_0006.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/jacs.4c09379&amp;id=_i1"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><p class="articleBody_abstractText">Mn-based fluorophosphates have attracted much attention as cathodes for sodium-ion batteries owing to their high cost effectiveness, considerable capacity, and stable framework. However, the fascinating Mn<sup>3+/2+</sup> redox couple suffers from inadequate activation due to the Mn–O covalent character and poor electronic conductivity, impeding its further applications. Herein, a local electronic structure regulation strategy is proposed to improve the Mn<sup>3+/2+</sup> redox potential and reversible capacity simultaneously through introducing elements with low-energy 3d orbitals to expand the energy gap between the e<sub>g</sub> orbitals and Fermi energy of Na. Moreover, the 3d element substitution serves to narrow the band gap toward the improved intrinsic electronic conductivity. In comparison with pristine Na<sub>2</sub>Fe<sub>0.45</sub>Mn<sub>0.55</sub>PO<sub>4</sub>F, the as-prepared Na<sub>2</sub>Fe<sub>0.45</sub>Mn<sub>0.4</sub>V<sub>0.1</sub>PO<sub>4</sub>F cathode achieves an increase from 3.5 to 3.6 V in the high-voltage platform and an improvement in energy density from 330 to 361 Wh kg<sup>–1</sup>. This work inspires new ideas in adjusting the redox potential of polyanionic cathodes through deliberate regulation of the local electronic structure.</p></div><div class="article__cc-license"><div class="article__cc-license__body"><p class="article__cc-license__para">This publication is licensed under the terms of your
                            institutional subscription.

                                        Article subjects are automatically applied from the ACS Subject Taxonomy and describe the scientific concepts and themes of the article.</p></div></header><ul class="article__tags__list"><li class="article__tags__item"><a class="article__tags__link" href="/action/doSearch?ConceptID=290687&amp;ref=fulltext" title="Electrical conductivity"> Electrical conductivity </a></li><li class="article__tags__item"><a class="article__tags__link" href="/action/doSearch?ConceptID=291371&amp;ref=fulltext" title="Electrodes"> Electrodes </a></li><li class="article__tags__item"><a class="article__tags__link" href="/action/doSearch?ConceptID=290692&amp;ref=fulltext" title="Elements"> Elements </a></li><li class="article__tags__item"><a class="article__tags__link" href="/action/doSearch?ConceptID=291719&amp;ref=fulltext" title="Redox reactions"> Redox reactions </a></li><li class="article__tags__item"><a class="article__tags__link" href="/action/doSearch?ConceptID=292316&amp;ref=fulltext" title="Transition metals"> Transition metals </a></li></ul></div></div></div></div><div class="article_content"><div class="article_content-table hlFld-FullText"><div class="article_content-row"><div class="article_content-left" property="articleBody" style="width: 384px;"><div id="sec1" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i2">1.  Introduction</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div class="NLM_p">Sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) have been demonstrated as a beneficial complement to lithium-ion batteries in the field of scalable energy storage, characterized by abundant sodium resources and their uniform distribution. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref1 ref2 ref3" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref1 ref2 ref3" aria-label="reference 1 to 3">(1−3)</a> The relatively higher redox potential of Na<sup>+</sup>/Na (−2.71 V) than that of Li<sup>+</sup>/Li (−3.04 V) urges the exploration of ideal cathode materials with higher redox potential to balance the energy density. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref4 ref5" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref4 ref5" aria-label="reference 4 and 5">(4,5)</a> Owing to the strong inductive effect of both polyanions and fluorine, fluorophosphates with the general formula of Na<sub>2</sub>MPO<sub>4</sub>F (M = Mn, Fe, Ni, Co, etc.) are emerging as potential high-voltage cathodes. Particularly, Na<sub>2</sub>MnPO<sub>4</sub>F with a fascinating theoretical redox potential of ∼3.66 V by the Mn<sup>3+/2+</sup> redox couple is believed to be a promising alternative. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref6" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref6" aria-label="reference 6">(6)</a></div><div class="NLM_p">However, Na<sub>2</sub>MnPO<sub>4</sub>F suffers from poor electrochemical activity due to its inherent low electronic and ionic conductivity. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref7 ref8 ref9 ref10" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref7 ref8 ref9 ref10" aria-label="reference 7 to 10">(7−10)</a> Partial substitution of Mn with Fe to obtain Na<sub>2</sub>Fe<sub><i>x</i></sub>Mn<sub>1–<i>x</i></sub>PO<sub>4</sub>F, kept in the three-dimensional tunnel structure at <i>x</i> &lt; 0.75, <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref7" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref7" aria-label="reference 7">(7)</a> has been demonstrated to improve the electrochemical activity of the Mn<sup>3+/2+</sup> redox couple. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref11 ref12 ref13" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref11 ref12 ref13" aria-label="reference 11 to 13">(11−13)</a> The porous Na<sub>2</sub>Fe<sub>0.6</sub>Mn<sub>0.4</sub>PO<sub>4</sub>F/C nanocomposite was reported with a capacity of 94.8 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> at 1/20C. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref14" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref14" aria-label="reference 14">(14)</a> Carbon-coated Na<sub>2</sub>Fe<sub>0.5</sub>Mn<sub>0.5</sub>PO<sub>4</sub>F exhibited a discharge capacity of 110 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> at 1/20C. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref15" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref15" aria-label="reference 15">(15)</a> Unfortunately, even at extremely low current densities, the redox potential of Mn<sup>3+/2+</sup> typically remains low, often below 3.5 V, or may even be unattainable. The underwhelming electrochemical performance of Mn<sup>3+/2+</sup> in this system seems to be intrinsic, which has prompted us to consider the electronic structure and orbital energy levels of Mn. It was postulated that a weaker M–O covalent bond reduces quantum mechanical repulsion between bonding and antibonding orbitals, resulting in an increased energy gap between the antibonding orbital and the Fermi level, thereby elevating the redox potential. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref11 ref17" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref11 ref17" aria-label="reference 11 and 17">(11,17)</a></div><div class="NLM_p last">Herein, inspired by the aforementioned, we propose a local electronic structure regulation strategy engendered by doping elements with low-energy d-orbital electrons to weaken the covalent character of the Mn–O bond, therefore enlarging the distance between e<sub>g</sub> orbitals of Mn and the Fermi energy of Na to elevate the Mn<sup>3+/2+</sup> redox potential. Furthermore, this strategy is characteristically effective for elements with d-orbital electrons (V<sup>3+</sup>: t<sub>2g</sub><sup>2</sup>e<sub>g</sub><sup>0</sup> and Cr<sup>3+</sup>: t<sub>2g</sub><sup>3</sup>e<sub>g</sub><sup>0</sup>), and elements without d-orbital electrons cannot regulate the redox potential (Al<sup>3+</sup>: [Ne] and Sc<sup>3+</sup>: [Ar]3d<sup>0</sup>). Upon doping V or Cr, the work function is increased, suggesting a higher energetic barrier for gaining electrons and thus leading to an increasing discharge voltage. For Na<sub>2</sub>Fe<sub>0.45</sub>Mn<sub>0.4</sub>V<sub>0.1</sub>PO<sub>4</sub>F (FMV-0.1), the Mn<sup>3+/2+</sup> potential platform can be almost fully realized for the first time, resulting in a significant increase in the discharge potential from 3.5 to 3.6 V, thereby boosting the energy density from 330 to 361 Wh kg<sup>–1</sup>. Additionally, FMV-0.1 demonstrates an excellent cycling stability of 1500 cycles at a 2C rate, with a capacity retention rate of 72%. This successful case underscores the pivotal role of electronic structure in electrochemical potential and indicates a new direction for research and optimization.</div></div><div id="sec2" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i3">2.  Results and Discussion</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div class="NLM_p">V<sup>3+</sup>(t<sub>2g</sub><sup>2</sup>e<sub>g</sub><sup>0</sup>), Cr<sup>3+</sup>(t<sub>2g</sub><sup>3</sup>e<sub>g</sub><sup>0</sup>), Al<sup>3+</sup>([Ne]), and Sc<sup>3+</sup>([Ar]3d<sup>0</sup>) are chosen as doping elements for pristine Na<sub>2</sub>Fe<sub>0.45</sub>Mn<sub>0.55</sub>PO<sub>4</sub>F in consideration of the different 3d orbital states. In terms of these trivalent elements, the Mn content is appropriately reduced to maintain electrical neutrality. Accordingly, Na<sub>2</sub>Fe<sub>0.45</sub>Mn<sub>0.55</sub>PO<sub>4</sub>F/C (FM), Na<sub>2</sub>Fe<sub>0.45</sub>Mn<sub>0.4</sub>V<sub>0.1</sub>PO<sub>4</sub>F/C (FMV-0.1), Na<sub>2</sub>Fe<sub>0.45</sub>Mn<sub>0.4</sub>Cr<sub>0.1</sub>PO<sub>4</sub>F/C (FMCr-0.1), Na<sub>2</sub>Fe<sub>0.45</sub>Mn<sub>0.4</sub>Sc<sub>0.1</sub>F/C (FMSc-0.1), and Na<sub>2</sub>Fe<sub>0.45</sub>Mn<sub>0.4</sub>Al<sub>0.1</sub>PO<sub>4</sub>F/C (FMAl-0.1) were all synthesized via a facile solid-state method.</div><div class="NLM_p">X-ray diffraction (XRD) was used to characterize the phase structures of the as-prepared samples (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 1</a>a), all of which exhibit high crystallinity and characteristic peaks well indexed to the monoclinic Na<sub>2</sub>MnPO<sub>4</sub>F structure (P2<sub>1</sub>/n, PDF card No. 97-005-9301). <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 1</a>b presents the schematic diagram of the FM cylindrical structure, illustrating a robust 3D open channel framework comprising the interlinkage of Fe/MnO<sub>4</sub>F<sub>2</sub> octahedra via F atoms along the <i>b</i>-axis and the connection of octahedra and PO<sub>4</sub> tetrahedra by sharing of O atoms in the ac-plane. Theoretical calculations reveal that the more stable TM atomic arrangement in FM is Fe and Mn atoms concentrating on the two sides of the supercell, respectively (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/jacs.4c09379/suppl_file/ja4c09379_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S1</a>). The crystallographic data of FM and FMV-0.1 were obtained by Rietveld refinement of XRD, as illustrated in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/jacs.4c09379/suppl_file/ja4c09379_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S2 and Table S1–2</a>. Because of the slightly smaller ionic radius of V<sup>3+</sup> than those of Mn<sup>2+</sup> and Fe<sup>2+</sup>, FMV-0.1 possesses a reduced cell volume of 858.956 Å<sup>3</sup> in contrast to FM (860.127 Å<sup>3</sup>). Raman spectra (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 1</a>c) reveal that all five samples show two characteristic carbon peaks of the D-band at 1350 cm<sup>–1</sup> and the G-band at 1590 cm<sup>–1</sup>, <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref18 ref19" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref18 ref19" aria-label="reference 18 and 19">(18,19)</a> the I<sub>D</sub>/I<sub>G</sub> ratios of which are basically equal at ∼0.88, indicating a similar partial graphitization degree of the carbon framework. Further, thermogravimetric analysis (TGA) in the range of 100–600 °C shows that the total mass losses of FM, FMV-0.1, FMCr-0.1, FMSc-0.1, and FMAl-0.1 are 10.4, 10.4, 9.6, 11.6, and 10.2 wt %, respectively (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/jacs.4c09379/suppl_file/ja4c09379_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S3</a>), implying the similar carbon content in all five samples.</div><figure id="fig1" data-index="1" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 1</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/jacs.4c09379/asset/images/medium/ja4c09379_0001.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/jacs.4c09379/asset/images/large/ja4c09379_0001.jpeg" id="gr1" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 1. (a) XRD patterns of FM and FM<i>X</i>-0.1 (<i>X</i> = V, Cr, Sc, and Al). (b) Schematic illustration of the monoclinic Na<sub>2</sub>MnPO<sub>4</sub>F structure visualized by VESTA. (c) Raman spectra and the corresponding I<sub>D</sub>/I<sub>G</sub> ratio of FM and FM<i>X</i>-0.1 (<i>X</i> = V, Cr, Sc, and Al). (d,e) HRTEM images (1), magnified HRTEM images (2), magnified images of the yellow square area (3), and SAED patterns (4) of FM and FMV-0.1. (f) High-angle annular dark field images and the relevant elemental mappings of FMV-0.1.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/jacs.4c09379/asset/images/large/ja4c09379_0001.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/jacs.4c09379&amp;id=fig1"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">Scanning electron microscopy (SEM) was used to observe the morphological features, as shown in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/jacs.4c09379/suppl_file/ja4c09379_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S4</a>. All the samples appear as irregular particles and display a similar size distribution within the range of a few hundred nanometers. High-resolution transmission electron microscopy (HRTEM) images (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 1</a>d1,e1) and their magnified images (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 1</a>d2,e2) show that the crystalline fluorophosphate grains are coated with an approximately 5 nm thick amorphous carbon layer. The magnified HRTEM images of the yellow square in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 1</a>d3,e3 can identify the interplanar spacings of 0.297 and 0.399 nm, which correspond to the (004) and (012) planes, respectively. Moreover, the selected-area electron diffraction (SAED) pattern (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 1</a>d4,e4) also confirms the high crystallinity of the as-prepared monoclinic-phase materials. Besides, energy-dispersive spectroscopy (EDS) indicates  that all elements within the FMV-0.1 particles are uniformly distributed (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 1</a>f).</div><div class="NLM_p">The electrochemical behaviors of all five samples were detected to evaluate the advantageous impact of the introduction of heteroatoms with d-orbital electrons. <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 2</a>a compares their galvanostatic charge–discharge (GCD) profiles at 0.1C. FM, FMV-0.1, FMCr-0.1, FMSc-0.1, and FMAl-0.1 deliver discharge capacities of 104.8, 114.8, 107.6, 96.0, and 93.6 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> at the second cycle, respectively. As <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 2</a>b depicts, all the samples display two well-defined charge/discharge platforms belonging to Fe<sup>3+/2+</sup> and Mn<sup>3+/2+</sup> redox couples, respectively, which is consistent with the two redox couples in the cyclic voltammetry (CV) curves. Both FMV-0.1 and FMCr-0.1 manifest a higher Mn<sup>3+/2+</sup> voltage platform at ∼3.6 V than FM. Consistently, the Mn<sup>3+/2+</sup> redox couples of FMV-0.1 and FMCr-0.1 are offset to higher voltages in the CV curves. As shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 2</a>c, the calculated Bader charges show that the partial charge of Mn decreased after the introduction of V<sup>3+</sup> and Cr<sup>3+</sup>, accompanied by the charge on the surrounding oxygen atoms increasing, which indicates a stronger ionic bond and weaker covalent bond characteristic of Mn–O. Mn 2p XPS spectra of FMV-0.1 and FMCr-0.1 shift to a higher binding energy than those of FM, reflecting the increase in the valence state of Mn, further confirming the enhanced ionic bond characteristic of Mn–O (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 2</a>d). All of the results indicate that the repulsion between the bonding and antibonding Mn orbitals reduces upon introducing V and Cr, resulting in an increased energy gap between the antibonding orbital and the Fermi level and thus an increasing redox potential. On the contrary, the introduction of Sc<sup>3+</sup> ([Ar]3d<sup>0</sup>) and Al<sup>3+</sup> ([Ne]) shows a recession of the voltage platform of the Mn<sup>3+/2+</sup> redox couple. The significantly smaller current density of the Mn<sup>3+/2+</sup> redox couple for FMAl-0.1 and FMSc-0.1, as depicted in the magnified CV curves, further confirms the results. As for the Fe<sup>3+/2+</sup> redox couple centered at around 2.9 V, all of the samples exhibit nearly the same voltage and capacity, which means that the introduction of heterogeneous elements has little effect on the redox of Fe. Besides, there is almost no shift in binding energy after V and Cr doping for the Fe 2p<sub>3/2</sub> peak (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/jacs.4c09379/suppl_file/ja4c09379_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S5</a>), which aligns with the GCD curves.</div><figure id="fig2" data-index="2" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 2</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/jacs.4c09379/asset/images/medium/ja4c09379_0002.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/jacs.4c09379/asset/images/large/ja4c09379_0002.jpeg" id="gr2" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 2. (a) GCD curves of FM and FM<i>X</i>-0.1 (<i>X</i> = V, Cr, Sc, and Al) SIBs at 0.1C. (b) CV curves of FM and FM<i>X</i>-0.1 (<i>X</i> = V, Cr, Sc, and Al) SIBs at a scan rate of 0.1 mV s<sup>–1</sup> and the magnified region of the Mn<sup>3+/2+</sup> redox couple. (c) Bader charge of Mn and O atoms of FM, FMV-0.1, and FMCr-0.1. (d) Mn 2p<sub>3/2</sub> XPS spectra of FM, FMV-0.1, and FMCr-0.1. (e) UPS spectra of FM, FMV-0.1, and FMSc-0.1. (f) Schematic energy level diagrams of FM and FMV-0.1. (g) PDOS of Na<sub>32</sub>Fe<sub>7</sub>Mn<sub>9</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>16</sub>F<sub>16</sub> and Na<sub>32</sub>Fe<sub>7</sub>Mn<sub>7</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>16</sub>F<sub>16</sub>. The Fermi level is set at 0 eV and is denoted as a black dashed line. The 3d orbital of Mn is shifted to lower energy, as depicted from a blue dashed line to a red dashed line.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/jacs.4c09379/asset/images/large/ja4c09379_0002.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/jacs.4c09379&amp;id=fig2"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">To more accurately explore the elevation mechanism of the Mn<sup>3+/2+</sup> platform, further analyses with ultraviolet photoelectron spectroscopy (UPS) and projected density of states (PDOS) were conducted to gain deep insights into the electronic structure. Detailedly, the samples for the UPS test were discharged to the end of the first platform (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/jacs.4c09379/suppl_file/ja4c09379_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S6a</a>). The cutoff energy (E<sub>cutoff</sub>) in bias voltage applied UPS spectra for FM, FMV-0.1, and FMSc-0.1 is measured to be 17.29, 17.18, and 19.15 eV, respectively. Based on the equation of Φ = hυ – |<i>E</i><sub>cutoff</sub> – <i>E</i><sub>f</sub>|, the corresponding work function (Φ) can be obtained as 3.91, 4.02, and 2.05 eV, respectively (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 2</a>e). The enlargement of Φ for FMV-0.1 denotes a higher energetic barrier for gaining electrons, leading to an increasing discharge voltage, as shown in GCD curves. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref20" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref20" aria-label="reference 20">(20)</a> As shown in the schematic diagram (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 2</a>f), this indicates that after the introduction of V<sup>3+</sup>, the energy of the d orbital (antibonding orbital) of Mn is lowered, and the gap between the Fermi level and the vacuum level increases. Conversely, for FMSc-0.1 and FMAl-0.1, doped with Sc without 3d electrons and Al without 3d orbitals, respectively, the work function is significantly reduced, and the corresponding GCD curves show a significantly degraded Mn<sup>3+/2+</sup> electrochemical platform (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/jacs.4c09379/suppl_file/ja4c09379_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S6b</a> and <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/jacs.4c09379/suppl_file/ja4c09379_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Table S3</a>). For FMCr-0.1, the Φ is slightly smaller than that of FM, which is consistent with the discharge curve as the FMCr-0.1 displays a more inclined platform than FM, resulting in a lower voltage at the end of the Mn<sup>3+/2+</sup> platform despite its higher average voltage. Besides, to gain further insight into the electronic structure and electrochemical properties of the samples, density functional theory (DFT) calculations  were employed, and the relevant crystal structures are given in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/jacs.4c09379/suppl_file/ja4c09379_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S7</a>. Aligning with the previous observation, the introduction of V and Cr makes the Mn 3d orbitals shift to lower energy (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figures 2</a>g and <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/jacs.4c09379/suppl_file/ja4c09379_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">S8</a>), leading to higher operating voltage. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref16" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref16" aria-label="reference 16">(16)</a></div><div class="NLM_p">Based on the crystal field theory, the energy levels of Mn 3d orbitals will split into low-energy t<sub>2g</sub> orbitals and high-energy e<sub>g</sub> orbitals in the octahedral field (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 3</a>a). The voltage delivered by Mn<sup>3+/2+</sup> depends on the distance between the e<sub>g</sub> orbitals and the Fermi energy level of Na. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref22" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref22" aria-label="reference 22">(22)</a> When the heteroatoms with d-orbital electrons replace the Mn site, such as V<sup>3+</sup> and Cr<sup>3+</sup>, the covalent character of Mn–O will be weakened. This weakening reduces the repulsion between the bonding and antibonding orbitals, thereby increasing the energy gap between the e<sub>g</sub> orbitals and the Fermi energy level and consequently elevating the redox potential. Sc<sup>3+</sup> ([Ar]3d<sup>0</sup>) and Al<sup>3+</sup> ([Ne]) are the elements without d-orbital electrons, signifying their inability to tailor the electronic structure and alter the discharge potential, which agrees well with the GCD curves presented in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 2</a>a.</div><figure id="fig3" data-index="3" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 3</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/jacs.4c09379/asset/images/medium/ja4c09379_0003.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/jacs.4c09379/asset/images/large/ja4c09379_0003.jpeg" id="gr3" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 3. (a) Crystal field splitting of Mn<sup>2+</sup> in octahedral coordination and schematic depicting the shift of Mn 3d orbitals when introducing different elements into the structure. (b) Plot of (Ahυ)<sup>2</sup> versus hυ of FM, FMV-0.1, and FMCr-0.1 according to UV–vis spectra; the inset is the corresponding band gap. (c) Electronic conductivity of FM and FM<i>X</i>-0.1 (X = V, Cr, Sc, and Al) tested by a four-point probe. (d) LSV curves of FM and FM<i>X</i>-0.1 (X = V, Cr, Sc, and Al). (e) GITT curves of FM, FMAl-0.1, and FMV-0.1.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/jacs.4c09379/asset/images/large/ja4c09379_0003.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/jacs.4c09379&amp;id=fig3"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">The effect of the kinetics was further evaluated, including electronic conductivity and Na<sup>+</sup> diffusivity. The UV–vis spectra of FM, FMV-0.1, and FMCr-0.1 were measured to calculate the band gap of the samples (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/jacs.4c09379/suppl_file/ja4c09379_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S9</a>). According to the Tauc plot, (αhυ)<sup>1/<i>n</i></sup> = <i>B</i>(<i>h</i>υ–E<sub>g</sub>), the corresponding (Ahυ)<sup>2</sup> hυ curves for band gap determination of FM, FMV-0.1, and FMCr-0.1 samples are shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 3</a>b. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref21" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref21" aria-label="reference 21">(21)</a> Noticeably, the band gap decreases from 3.09 eV (FM) to 3.00 eV (FMV-0.1) and 2.89 eV (FMCr-0.1), indicating an enhanced electronic conductivity after V and Cr doping. Furthermore, Kelvin four-terminal sensing was further used to measure the electronic conductivity (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 3</a>c). Consistent with the UV–vis tests, FMV-0.1 and FMCr-0.1 display the electronic conductivity of 7.69 × 10<sup>–5</sup> and 1.00 × 10<sup>–4</sup> S m<sup>–1</sup>, respectively, which are almost 4 times and 5 times higher than that of FM (1.92 × 10<sup>–5</sup> S m<sup>–1</sup>). However, for FMAl-0.1 with the element without d orbitals, the electronic conductivity (1.16 × 10<sup>–5</sup> S m<sup>–1</sup>) is lower than that of FM. And for FMSc-0.1, doped with the element Sc possessing 3d empty orbitals, the electronic conductivity is comparable to that of FM, far less than those of FMV-0.1 and FMCr-0.1. The conductivity regularity of linear sweep voltammetry (LSV) curves is consistent with the four-terminal data (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 3</a>d). Galvanostatic intermittent titration technique (GITT) was carried out to obtain the Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion coefficient to study the electrochemical kinetics. The Mn<sup>3+/2+</sup> redox voltage platforms of FM and FMAl-0.1 electrodes display similar distributions of Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion coefficients, and the average Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion coefficients within the voltage plateau range are low, approximately ∼9.2 × 10<sup>–12</sup> cm<sup>2</sup> s<sup>–1</sup> and ∼1.3 × 10<sup>–11</sup> cm<sup>2</sup> s<sup>–1</sup>, respectively. In contrast, the Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion coefficient of FMV-0.1 at the Mn<sup>3+/2+</sup> redox voltage platform experiences a significant increase, with an average Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion coefficient of about 1.6 × 10<sup>–11</sup> cm<sup>2</sup> s<sup>–1</sup> (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figures 3</a>e and <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/jacs.4c09379/suppl_file/ja4c09379_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">S10</a>). The rapid Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion kinetics contribute to the higher reversible capacity exhibited by FMV-0.1. The interfacial kinetics of the Na<sup>+</sup> transfer process were further analyzed via electrochemical impedance spectroscopy (EIS), and the trend of charge transfer impedance (<i>R</i><sub>ct</sub>) well aligns with the results of LSV and GITT (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/jacs.4c09379/suppl_file/ja4c09379_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S11</a> and <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/jacs.4c09379/suppl_file/ja4c09379_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Table S4</a>).</div><div class="NLM_p">In situ XRD was employed to fully analyze the phase transition process of FMV-0.1 in the initial cycle. The main peaks of (1 1 1), (0 1 2), (2 1 0), (−4 0 2), (4 0 0), (0 2 0), and (0 1 4) can be clearly observed and indexed (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 4</a>a2,b2). The position of Bragg peaks of both FM and FMV-0.1 electrodes exhibits continuous shifts throughout the charge/discharge process, with no emergence of new peaks. This observation presents a single-phase solid solution Na<sup>+</sup> intercalation/extraction process, which is consistent with the previous reports. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref23" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref23" aria-label="reference 23">(23)</a> Noticeably, the Na-poor state in the high voltage region will widen the Bragg peaks, as shown by the blue and red rectangle regions. It can be observed that the peak broadening region of FMV-0.1 is longer than that of FM, which means more sodium extraction in FMV-0.1 (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 4</a>a3,b3). This corresponds to its more adequate Mn<sup>2+/3+</sup> redox process and higher specific capacity. The stacked in situ XRD patterns in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 4</a>a4,b4 show the same tendency, with a bigger shift of the peak position in FMV-0.1 than that in FM.</div><figure id="fig4" data-index="4" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 4</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/jacs.4c09379/asset/images/medium/ja4c09379_0004.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/jacs.4c09379/asset/images/large/ja4c09379_0004.jpeg" id="gr4" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 4. (a,b) In situ XRD patterns of FM and FMV-0.1 showing structural evolution during cycling in the voltage window of 2.0–4.2 V. (c) Rate performance comparison of FM and FM<i>X</i>-0.1 (<i>X</i> = V, Cr, Sc, and Al) SIBs. (d) Cost/energy performance of FMV-0.1 and other typical polyanion cathodes for SIBs. Cycling performance of FM and FM<i>X</i>-0.1 (<i>X</i> = V, Cr, Sc, and Al) at 2C.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/jacs.4c09379/asset/images/large/ja4c09379_0004.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/jacs.4c09379&amp;id=fig4"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">The rate capability comparison highlights the superiority of FMV-0.1 and FMCr-0.1 (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 4</a>c), driven by the aforementioned enhanced electronic conductivity. The reversible capacities of 116.1, 107.7, 96.3, 85.7, 73.7, 57.4, and 45.1 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> at various current rates of 0.1, 0.2, 0.5, 1, 2, 5, and 10C, respectively, are achieved for FMV-0.1, and a capacity of 112.0 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> can be recovered when the current rate was turned back to 0.1C. The improved capacity of 116.1 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> and the high Mn<sup>3+/2+</sup> operating voltage of 3.6 V of FMV-0.1 give rise to a remarkable energy density of approximately 361 Wh kg<sup>–1</sup> at 0.1C. Thereafter, the raw materials cost per energy of FMV-0.1 was estimated (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/jacs.4c09379/suppl_file/ja4c09379_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Table S5</a> and <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 4</a>d), which reflects a cost advantage over the reported polyanion cathodes for SIBs, manifesting great practical application prospects. Moreover, FMV-0.1 and FMCr-0.1 display a stable cycling performance, as shown in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/jacs.4c09379/suppl_file/ja4c09379_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figures S12</a> and <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">4</a>e. For FMCr-0.1, the initial discharge specific capacity is 85 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> at 0.5C, surpassing the specific capacity of FM (79 mAh g<sup>–1</sup>). In addition, the FMCr-0.1 cathode maintains a high capacity of 73 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> and a remarkable capacity retention rate of 86% after 400 cycles, whereas FM retains only 61 mAh g<sup>–1</sup>. Furthermore, FMV-0.1 and FMCr-0.1 also exhibit outstanding cycling stability, with 72% and 77% capacity retention, respectively, after 1500 cycles at 2C, which is much better than that of FM (60%).</div><div class="NLM_p">To further demonstrate the versatility of FMV-0.1, the pouch cell was assembled with hard carbon (HC) and FMV-0.1 as the anode and cathode, respectively, and is labeled FMV-0.1//HC. <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 5</a>a gives a schematic diagram of the cell structure. The sodium storage behavior of the HC anode and FMV-0.1 cathode was tested (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 5</a>b). Accordingly, the discharge capacity is 98.3 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> (based on the cathode) during the optimized voltage range of 1.5–4.0 V, thus achieving a high energy density of 306.9 Wh kg<sup>–1</sup> (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 5</a>c). Additionally, an outstanding cycling performance with 77% capacity retention after 235 cycles and 75% capacity retention after 450 cycles is achieved at 0.5 and 1C, respectively (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 5</a>e).</div><figure id="fig5" data-index="5" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 5</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/jacs.4c09379/asset/images/medium/ja4c09379_0005.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/jacs.4c09379/asset/images/large/ja4c09379_0005.jpeg" id="gr5" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 5. (a) Schematic configuration of the FMV-0.1//HC sodium-ion full cell. (b) GCD curves of the FMV-0.1 cathode and HC anode. (c) GCD curve of the FMV-0.1//HC pouch cell. (d) Optical photograph of the LED band lighted up by the FMV-0.1//HC pouch cell. (e) Cycling stability of the FMV-0.1//HC pouch cell at 0.5 and 1C.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/jacs.4c09379/asset/images/large/ja4c09379_0005.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/jacs.4c09379&amp;id=fig5"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure></div><div id="sec3" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i4">3.  Conclusions</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div class="NLM_p last">In summary, a local electronic structure regulation strategy is brought forward by introducing elements with different d-orbital states. V<sup>3+</sup> or Cr<sup>3+</sup> substitution results in a weaker covalent character of the Mn–O bond and induces the enlargement of the energy gap between e<sub>g</sub> orbitals of Mn<sup>2+</sup> and the Fermi energy of the Na metal, leading to a stronger energetic barrier for gaining electrons. Furthermore, the significant reduction in the band gap promotes the enhancement of intrinsic electronic conductivity. As compared to FM, the obtained FMV-0.1 exhibits an evident elevation of the discharge platform from 3.5 to 3.6 V, with an enhanced energy density from 330 to 361 Wh kg<sup>–1</sup>. This work not only proposes a novel strategy to rationally regulate the discharge voltage of Mn by tailoring the d-orbital electrons but also sheds light on the design of cathode materials with the desired electrochemical potential for high-performance SIBs.</div></div><div class="NLM_back"><div class="article_supporting-info" id="notes1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i5">Supporting Information</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i electrochemical performance of the batteries, and various characterizations including XRD Rietveld refinement, SEM, TGA, XPS results, EIS measurement, GCD curves, UPS spectra, UV–vis spectra, and         </div><div class="casAbstract">Electrochem. properties of A2MnPO4F (A = Na/Li) were investigated both exptl. and with first principles calcns.  A new Li2MnPO4F phase was successfully synthesized for the first time.  One alkali metal ion reaction occurred reversibly within a reasonable voltage window and a two alkali metal ion reaction took place at higher voltages.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref7"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref7" class="refNumLink">7</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit7" data-doi="10.1149/1.3236480"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Recham, N.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Chotard, J.-N.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Dupont, L.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Djellab, K.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Armand, M.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Tarascon, J.-M.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Ionothermal synthesis of sodium-based fluorophosphate cathode materials</span>. <i>J. Electrochem. Soc.</i>  <span class="NLM_year" style="font-weight: bold;">2009</span>,  <span class="NLM_volume"><i>156</i></span> (<span class="NLM_issue">12</span>),  <span class="NLM_fpage">A993</span>– <span class="NLM_lpage">A999</span>, <span class="refDoi">&nbsp;DOI:

                        cas_cit7R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">7</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Ionothermal Synthesis of Sodium-Based Fluorophosphate Cathode Materials</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Recham, N.; Chotard, J.-N.; Dupont, L.; Djellab, K.; Armand, M.; Tarascon, J.-M.</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Journal of the Electrochemical Society</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2009</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">156</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">12</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">A993-A999</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">JESOAN</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">0013-4651</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Electrochemical Society</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Owing to cost and abundance considerations, Na-based electrode materials are regaining interest, esp. those that can be prepd. at low temps.  The low-temp. synthesis of highly divided Na-based fluorophosphates (Na2MPO4F, M = Fe, Mn, or mixts.) in ionic liq. media is presented.  The ionothermal approach enables the synthesis of these phases at temps. ≥270°, while temps. ≤600°C are needed to obtain similar quality phases by solid-state reactions.  Due to their highly divided character, Na2FePO4F powders made via this process show better electrochem. performances vs. either Li or Na than their ceramic counterparts.  Regardless of how they were made, the Na2MnPO4F powders, which crystallize in a 3-dimensional (3D) tunnel structure rather than in the 2-dimensional (2D)-layered structure of Na2FePO4F, were poorly electroactive.  Substituting 0.25 Fe for Mn in Na2Fe1-xMnxPO4F is sufficient to trigger a 2-dimensional-3D structural transition and leads to rapid decay of the materials electrochem. performances.  A tentative explanation, based on structural considerations to account for such behavior, is given.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref8"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref8" class="refNumLink">8</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit8" data-doi="10.1039/C4RA05336B"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Lin, X.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Hou, X.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wu, X.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wang, S.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Gao, M.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Yang, Y.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Exploiting Na<sub>2</sub>MnPO<sub>4</sub>F as a high-capacity and well-reversible cathode material for Na-ion batteries</span>. <i>RSC Adv.</i>  <span class="NLM_year" style="font-weight: bold;">2014</span>,  <span class="NLM_volume"><i>4</i></span>,  <span class="NLM_fpage">40985</span>– <span class="NLM_lpage">40993</span>, <span class="refDoi">&nbsp;DOI:

        </div><div class="casAbstract">A Na2MnPO4F/C nanocomposite material is successfully synthesized via spray drying, followed by a high-temp. sintering method.  It is shown that the highly phase-pure Na2MnPO4F with symmetry of the P21/n space group is uniformly embedded in the carbon networks, which play a key role in building up a highly efficient, electron-flow channel and elevating the electronic cond. of the nanocomposites.  The electrochem. measurements show that the initial discharge capacity of Na2MnPO4F reaches up to 140 and 178 mA h g-1 at 30 °C and 55 °C, resp.  Furthermore, the capacity still maintains 135 mA h g-1 after 20 cycles at 55 °C.  The Na+ diffusion coeff. in Na2MnPO4F is calcd. at about 10-17 cm2 s-1 by the GITT method.         </div><div class="casAbstract">A review. In analogy to lithium-ion batteries, the cost of EESSs depends mainly on charge-carrier ions and redox centers in electrodes, and their performance is limited by pos. electrodes. (MPCs)-a family with robust frameworks and large channels for ion storage and migration.                         cas_cit13R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">13</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">A Novel NASICON-Typed Na4VMn0.5Fe0.5(PO4)3 Cathode for High-Performance Na-Ion Batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Xu, Chunliu; Zhao, Junmei; Wang, Enhui; Liu, Xiaohong; Shen, Xing; Rong, Xiaohui; Zheng, Qiong; Ren, Guoxin; Zhang, Nian; Liu, Xiaosong; Guo, Xiaodong; Yang, Chao; Liu, Huizhou; Zhong, Benhe; Hu, Yong-Sheng</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Advanced Energy Materials</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2021</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">11</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">22</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">2100729</span>CODEN:

        </div><div class="casAbstract">The Na+ superionic conductor (NASICON)-type Na3V2(PO4)3 cathodes have attracted extensive interest due to their high structural stability and fast Na+ mobility.  However, the substitution of vanadium with low-cost active elements remains imperative due to high cost of vanadium, to further boost its application feasibility.  Herein, a novel ternary NASICON-type Na4VMn0.5Fe0.5(PO4)3/C cathode is designed, which integrates the advantages of large reversible capacity, high voltage, and good stability.  The as-obtained Na4VMn0.5Fe0.5(PO4)3/C composite can deliver an excellent rate capacity of 96 m Ah g-1 at 20 C and decent cycling durability of 94% after 3000 cycles at 20 C, which is superior to that of Na4VFe(PO4)3/C and Na4VMn(PO4)3/C.  The synergetic contributions of multimetal ions and facilitated Na+ migration of the Na4VMn0.5Fe0.5(PO4)3/C cathode are confirmed by the first-principles calcns.  The processive redn./oxidn. involved in Fe2+/Fe3+, Mn2+/Mn3+, V3+/V4+/V5+ redox couples are also revealed upon the charging/discharging process by ex situ soft X-ray absorption spectroscopy.  The reversible structure evolution and small vol. change during the electrochem. reaction is demonstrated by in situ X-ray diffraction characterization.                         cas_cit16R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">16</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Local electronic structure modulation enhances operating voltage in Li-rich cathodes</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Sun, Gang; Yu, Fu-Da; Que, Lan-Fang; Deng, Liang; Wang, Min-Jun; Jiang, Yun-Shan; Shao, Guangjie; Wang, Zhen-Bo</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Nano Energy</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2019</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">66</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue"></span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">104102</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">NEANCA</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">2211-2855</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Elsevier Ltd.</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Lithium-rich materials, which exhibit high capacity due to the existence of anionic redox, are in highly demanded for high energy d. Li ion batteries.  Nevertheless, the low operating voltage and rapid voltage drop of Li-Rich cathodes are seen as the fatal issues, which result in reduced energy d. and degraded energy quality of the batteries, limiting their further com. application.  Herein, we focus on the local electronic structure controlled by chem. compn. (to prepd. high Ni content Li-rich cathode) to improve the operating voltage and suppress voltage decay.  Through detailed phys. characterization, electrochem. anal. and theor. calcns., we summarize three main factors for improving the voltage of lithium-rich materials: 1) Increasing the O-Ni2+/3+/Li+ content effectively regulates the coordination environment of oxygen in Li-rich materials, realizes the regulation of local electronic structure; 2) the substitution of Ni increases the oxidn. state of TM ions, esp. for Mn, to keep charge balance, so as to improve the operating voltage and suppress voltage decay; 3) adjusting local electronic structure shifts the TM 3d-O 2p bands and the non-bonding O 2p band to lower energy, leading to an increase in the redox reaction potential.  This strategy provides a method to regulate the coupling reaction of cation and anion redox in Li-rich cathodes by compositional design, so as to realize the regulation of voltage.  Furthermore, when the as-prepd. high Ni content electrode assembled with Si/C anode into a full-cell, impressive operating voltage (∼3.70 V) and long cycle life (72.29% capacity retention after 200 cycles at 1C) can be achieved.  Our work provides an efficient and up-scalable strategy for the prepn. of electrodes with high voltage and high useful energy d., and making them a rather promising cathode candidate for Li ion batteries.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref17"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref17" class="refNumLink">17</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit17" data-doi="10.1002/adma.202305135"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Hao, Z.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Shi, X.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Yang, Z.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhou, X.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Li, L.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Ma, C.
